<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Cell (biology)</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Basic unit of all known organisms</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the basic unit of lifeforms. For the branch of biology that studies them, see <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">Cell biology</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="background-color:dimgray; color: white">Cell</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilson1900Fig2.jpg"><img alt="Wilson1900Fig2.jpg" data-file-height="1545" data-file-width="2211" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Wilson1900Fig2.jpg/250px-Wilson1900Fig2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Wilson1900Fig2.jpg/375px-Wilson1900Fig2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Wilson1900Fig2.jpg/500px-Wilson1900Fig2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a><div class="infobox-caption"><a href="/wiki/Onion" title="Onion">Onion</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allium_cepa" title="Allium cepa">Allium cepa</a></i>) root cells in different phases of the <a href="/wiki/Cell_cycle" title="Cell cycle">cell cycle</a> (drawn by <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Beecher_Wilson" title="Edmund Beecher Wilson">E. B. width="250"/></a><div class="infobox-caption">A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> cell (left) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">prokaryotic</a> cell (right)</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #efefef">Identifiers</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" href="https://www4.unifr.ch/ifaa/Public/EntryPage/PDF/TH%20Chapter%20H1.00.pdf" rel="nofollow">H1.00.01.0.00001  </a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.25em"><a href="/wiki/Foundational_Model_of_Anatomy" title="Foundational Model of Anatomy">FMA</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="external text" href="https://bioportal.bioontology.org/ontologies/FMA/?p=classes&amp;conceptid=http%3A%2F%2Fpurl.org%2Fsig%2Font%2Ffma%2Ffma686465" rel="nofollow">686465</a></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-below" colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terminology" title="Anatomical terminology">Anatomical terminology</a><div style="text-align: right;"><small class="noprint">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q7868" title="d:Q7868">edit on Wikidata</a>]</small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>cell</b> (from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cellula</i></span> meaning 'small room'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr12_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-npr12-1">[1]</a></sup>) is the basic structural and functional unit of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_form" title="Life form">life forms</a>. Every cell consists of a <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> enclosed within a <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">membrane</a>, which contains many <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts20022_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts20022-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cells can acquire specified function and carry out various tasks within the cell such as replication, DNA repair, protein synthesis, and motility. Cells are capable of specialization and mobility within the cell. Most cells are measured in micrometers due to their small size.
</p><p>Most plant and animal cells are only visible under a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_microscope" title="Light microscope">light microscope</a>, with dimensions between 1 and 100 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_microscopy" title="Electron microscopy">Electron microscopy</a> gives a much higher resolution showing greatly detailed cell structure. Organisms can be classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicellular" title="Unicellular">unicellular</a> (consisting of a single cell such as <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> (including plants and animals).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> Most <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">unicellular organisms</a> are classed as <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>. The number of cells in plants and animals varies from species to species; it has been approximated that the human body contains an estimated 37 trillion (3.72×10<sup>13</sup>) cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The brain accounts for around 80 billion of these cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JCN2_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-JCN2-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The study of cells and how they work has led to many other studies in related areas of biology, including: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_of_DNA" title="Discovery of DNA">discovery of DNA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cancer_systems_biology" title="Cancer systems biology">cancer systems biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ageing" title="Ageing">aging</a> and <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental biology</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">Cell biology</a> is the study of cells, which were discovered by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> in 1665, who named them for their resemblance to <a href="/wiki/Monastic_cell" title="Monastic cell">cells</a> inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Christian_monasticism" title="Christian monasticism">Christian monks</a> in a monastery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karp20092_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karp20092-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cell_theory" title="Cell theory">Cell theory</a>, first developed in 1839 by <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Jakob_Schleiden" title="Matthias Jakob Schleiden">Matthias Jakob Schleiden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Schwann" title="Theodor Schwann">Theodor Schwann</a>, states that all organisms are composed of one or more cells, that cells are the fundamental unit of structure and function in all living organisms, and that all cells come from pre-existing cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Cells emerged on Earth about 4 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin12_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin12-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin22_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin22-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RavenJohnson20022_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-RavenJohnson20022-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell_types">Cell types</span></h2>
<p>Cells are of two types: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a>, which contain a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">prokaryotic cells</a>, which do not have a nucleus, but a nucleoid region is still present. Prokaryotes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-celled_organism" title="Single-celled organism">single-celled organisms</a>, while eukaryotes may be either single-celled or <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prokaryotic_cells">Prokaryotic cells</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">Prokaryote</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prokaryote_cell.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="910" decoding="async" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Prokaryote_cell.svg/280px-Prokaryote_cell.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Prokaryote_cell.svg/420px-Prokaryote_cell.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Prokaryote_cell.svg/560px-Prokaryote_cell.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prokaryote_cell.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a typical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">prokaryotic</a> cell</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">Prokaryotes</a> include <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a>, two of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_domain_system" title="Three domain system">three</a> <a href="/wiki/Domain_(biology)" title="Domain (biology)">domains of life</a>. Prokaryotic cells were the first form of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> on Earth, characterized by having vital <a href="/wiki/Biological_process" title="Biological process">biological processes</a> including <a href="/wiki/Cell_signaling" title="Cell signaling">cell signaling</a>. They are simpler and smaller than eukaryotic cells, and lack a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>, and other membrane-bound <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a>. The <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> of a prokaryotic cell consists of a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circular_prokaryote_chromosome" title="Circular prokaryote chromosome">circular chromosome</a> that is in direct contact with the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a>. The nuclear region in the cytoplasm is called the <a href="/wiki/Nucleoid" title="Nucleoid">nucleoid</a>. Most prokaryotes are the smallest of all organisms ranging from 0.5 to 2.0 μm in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>A prokaryotic cell has three regions:
</p>
<ul><li>Enclosing the cell is the <a href="/wiki/Cell_envelope" title="Cell envelope">cell envelope</a> – generally consisting of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane" title="Plasma membrane">plasma membrane</a> covered by a <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a> which, for some bacteria, may be further covered by a third layer called a <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_capsule" title="Bacterial capsule">capsule</a>. Though most prokaryotes have both a cell membrane and a cell wall, there are exceptions such as <i><a href="/wiki/Mycoplasma" title="Mycoplasma">Mycoplasma</a></i> (bacteria) and <i><a href="/wiki/Thermoplasma" title="Thermoplasma">Thermoplasma</a></i> (archaea) which only possess the cell membrane layer. The envelope gives rigidity to the cell and separates the interior of the cell from its environment, serving as a protective filter. The cell wall consists of <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycan</a> in bacteria and acts as an additional barrier against exterior forces. It also prevents the cell from expanding and bursting (<a href="/wiki/Cytolysis" title="Cytolysis">cytolysis</a>) from <a href="/wiki/Osmotic_pressure" title="Osmotic pressure">osmotic pressure</a> due to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypotonic" title="Hypotonic">hypotonic</a> environment. Some eukaryotic cells (<a href="/wiki/Plant_cell" title="Plant cell">plant cells</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungal" title="Fungal">fungal</a> cells) also have a cell wall.</li>
<li>Inside the cell is the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasmic region</a> that contains the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> (DNA), ribosomes and various sorts of inclusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> The genetic material is freely found in the cytoplasm. Prokaryotes can carry <a href="/wiki/Extrachromosomal_DNA" title="Extrachromosomal DNA">extrachromosomal DNA</a> elements called <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmids</a>, which are usually circular. Linear bacterial plasmids have been identified in several species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirochete" title="Spirochete">spirochete</a> bacteria, including members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Borrelia" title="Borrelia">Borrelia</a></i> notably <i><a href="/wiki/Borrelia_burgdorferi" title="Borrelia burgdorferi">Borrelia burgdorferi</a></i>, which causes Lyme disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Though not forming a nucleus, the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> is condensed in a <a href="/wiki/Nucleoid" title="Nucleoid">nucleoid</a>. Plasmids encode additional genes, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a> genes.</li>
<li>On the outside, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagella" title="Flagella">flagella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pilus" title="Pilus">pili</a> project from the cell's surface. These are structures (not present in all prokaryotes) made of proteins that facilitate movement and communication between cells.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animal_cell_structure_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="484" data-file-width="724" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Animal_cell_structure_en.svg/280px-Animal_cell_structure_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Animal_cell_structure_en.svg/420px-Animal_cell_structure_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Animal_cell_structure_en.svg/560px-Animal_cell_structure_en.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Animal_cell_structure_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a typical animal cell</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plant_cell_structure-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="475" data-file-width="649" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Plant_cell_structure-en.svg/280px-Plant_cell_structure-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Plant_cell_structure-en.svg/420px-Plant_cell_structure-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Plant_cell_structure-en.svg/560px-Plant_cell_structure-en.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plant_cell_structure-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of a typical <a href="/wiki/Plant_cell" title="Plant cell">plant cell</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eukaryotic_cells">Eukaryotic cells</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">Eukaryote</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">Plants</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animals" title="Animals">animals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slime_mould" title="Slime mould">slime moulds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> are all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a>. These cells are about fifteen times wider than a typical prokaryote and can be as much as a thousand times greater in volume. The main distinguishing feature of eukaryotes as compared to prokaryotes is <a href="/wiki/Cellular_compartment" title="Cellular compartment">compartmentalization</a>: the presence of membrane-bound <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a> (compartments) in which specific activities take place. Most important among these is a <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> an organelle that houses the cell's <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>. This nucleus gives the eukaryote its name, which means "true kernel (nucleus)". Some of the other differences are:
</p>
<ul><li>The plasma membrane resembles that of prokaryotes in function, with minor differences in the setup. Cell walls may or may not be present.</li>
<li>The eukaryotic DNA is organized in one or more linear molecules, called <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>, which are associated with <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">histone</a> proteins. All chromosomal DNA is stored in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a>, separated from the cytoplasm by a membrane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> Some eukaryotic organelles such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> also contain some DNA.</li>
<li>Many eukaryotic cells are <a href="/wiki/Cilium" title="Cilium">ciliated</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_cilia" title="Primary cilia">primary cilia</a>. Primary cilia play important roles in chemosensation, <a href="/wiki/Mechanosensation" title="Mechanosensation">mechanosensation</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermosensation" title="Thermosensation">thermosensation</a>. Each cilium may thus be "viewed as a sensory cellular <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(biology)" title="Antenna (biology)">antennae</a> that coordinates a large number of cellular signaling pathways, sometimes coupling the signaling to ciliary motility or alternatively to cell division and differentiation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christenson2008_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christenson2008-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li>Motile eukaryotes can move using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motile_cilia" title="Motile cilia">motile cilia</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagella" title="Flagella">flagella</a>. Motile cells are absent in <a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raven_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-raven-18">[18]</a></sup> Eukaryotic flagella are more complex than those of prokaryotes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li></ul>
