<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell_shapes">Cell shapes</span></h2>
<p>Cell shape, also called cell morphology, has been hypothesized to form from the arrangement and movement of the cytoskeleton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Many advancements in the study of cell morphology come from studying simple bacteria such as <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i>,  <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i>,  and <a href="/wiki/Bacillus_subtilis" title="Bacillus subtilis"><i>B. subtilis</i>.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">[22]</a></sup> Different cell shapes have been found and described, but how and why cells form different shapes is still widely unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-22">[22]</a></sup> Some cell shapes that have been identified include rods, cocci and spirochaetes. Cocci have a circular shape, bacilli have an elongated rod-like shape, and spirochaetes have a spiral shape. Many other shapes have also been determined. 
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Subcellular_components">Subcellular components</span></h2>
<p>All cells, whether <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">prokaryotic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a>, have a <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">membrane</a> that envelops the cell, regulates what moves in and out (selectively permeable), and maintains the <a href="/wiki/Membrane_potential" title="Membrane potential">electric potential of the cell</a>. Inside the membrane, the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> takes up most of the cell's volume. Except <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, which lack a cell nucleus and most organelles to accommodate maximum space for <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>, all cells possess <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>, the hereditary material of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, containing the information necessary to <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">build</a> various <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>, the cell's primary machinery. There are also other kinds of <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biomolecules</a> in cells. This article lists these primary <a href="/wiki/Cellular_component" title="Cellular component">cellular components</a>, then briefly describes their function.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell_membrane">Cell membrane</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">Cell membrane</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cell_membrane_detailed_diagram_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="877" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Cell_membrane_detailed_diagram_en.svg/300px-Cell_membrane_detailed_diagram_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Cell_membrane_detailed_diagram_en.svg/450px-Cell_membrane_detailed_diagram_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Cell_membrane_detailed_diagram_en.svg/600px-Cell_membrane_detailed_diagram_en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cell_membrane_detailed_diagram_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detailed diagram of lipid bilayer of cell membrane</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a>, or plasma membrane, is a selectively permeable<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biological_membrane" title="Biological membrane">biological membrane</a> that surrounds the cytoplasm of a cell. In animals, the plasma membrane is the outer boundary of the cell, while in plants and prokaryotes it is usually covered by a <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a>. This membrane serves to separate and protect a cell from its surrounding environment and is made mostly from a <a href="/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">double layer of phospholipids</a>, which are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphiphilic" title="Amphiphilic">amphiphilic</a> (partly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> and partly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a>). Hence, the layer is called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phospholipid_bilayer" title="Phospholipid bilayer">phospholipid bilayer</a>, or sometimes a fluid mosaic membrane. Embedded within this membrane is a macromolecular structure called the <a href="/wiki/Porosome" title="Porosome">porosome</a> the universal secretory portal in cells and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> molecules that act as channels and pumps that move different molecules into and out of the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> The membrane is semi-permeable, and selectively permeable, in that it can either let a substance (<a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecule</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a>) pass through freely, pass through to a limited extent or not pass through at all. Cell surface membranes also contain <a href="/wiki/Receptor_(biochemistry)#Transmembrane_receptors" title="Receptor (biochemistry)">receptor</a> proteins that allow cells to detect external signaling molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cytoskeleton">Cytoskeleton</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cytoskeleton" title="Cytoskeleton">Cytoskeleton</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DAPIMitoTrackerRedAlexaFluor488BPAE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1040" data-file-width="1384" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/DAPIMitoTrackerRedAlexaFluor488BPAE.jpg/220px-DAPIMitoTrackerRedAlexaFluor488BPAE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/DAPIMitoTrackerRedAlexaFluor488BPAE.jpg/330px-DAPIMitoTrackerRedAlexaFluor488BPAE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/DAPIMitoTrackerRedAlexaFluor488BPAE.jpg/440px-DAPIMitoTrackerRedAlexaFluor488BPAE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DAPIMitoTrackerRedAlexaFluor488BPAE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A fluorescent image of an endothelial cell. Nuclei are stained blue, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> are stained red, and microfilaments are stained green.</div></div></div>
<p>The cytoskeleton acts to organize and maintain the cell's shape; anchors organelles in place; helps during <a href="/wiki/Endocytosis" title="Endocytosis">endocytosis</a>, the uptake of external materials by a cell, and <a href="/wiki/Cytokinesis" title="Cytokinesis">cytokinesis</a>, the separation of daughter cells after <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a>; and moves parts of the cell in processes of growth and mobility. The eukaryotic cytoskeleton is composed of <a href="/wiki/Microtubule" title="Microtubule">microtubules</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_filament" title="Intermediate filament">intermediate filaments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microfilament" title="Microfilament">microfilaments</a>. In the cytoskeleton of a <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neuron</a> the intermediate filaments are known as <a href="/wiki/Neurofilament" title="Neurofilament">neurofilaments</a>. There are a great number of proteins associated with them, each controlling a cell's structure by directing, bundling, and aligning filaments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> The prokaryotic cytoskeleton is less well-studied but is involved in the maintenance of cell shape, <a href="/wiki/Cell_polarity" title="Cell polarity">polarity</a> and cytokinesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The subunit protein of microfilaments is a small, monomeric protein called <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a>. The subunit of microtubules is a dimeric molecule called <a href="/wiki/Tubulin" title="Tubulin">tubulin</a>. Intermediate filaments are heteropolymers whose subunits vary among the cell types in different tissues. Some of the subunit proteins of intermediate filaments include <a href="/wiki/Vimentin" title="Vimentin">vimentin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desmin" title="Desmin">desmin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamin" title="Lamin">lamin</a> (lamins A, B and C), <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a> (multiple acidic and basic keratins), and neurofilament proteins (NF–L, NF–M).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_material">Genetic material</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DNA_orbit_animated.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="477" data-file-width="290" decoding="async" height="362" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/DNA_orbit_animated.gif/220px-DNA_orbit_animated.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/DNA_orbit_animated.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DNA_orbit_animated.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">Deoxyribonucleic acid</a> (DNA)</div></div></div>
<p>Two different kinds of genetic material exist: <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">deoxyribonucleic acid</a> (DNA) and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">ribonucleic acid</a> (RNA). Cells use DNA for their long-term information storage. The biological information contained in an organism is <a href="/wiki/Genetic_code" title="Genetic code">encoded</a> in its DNA sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> RNA is used for information transport (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRNA" title="MRNA">mRNA</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymatic</a> functions (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomal</a> RNA). <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">Transfer RNA</a> (tRNA) molecules are used to add amino acids during protein <a href="/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translation</a>.
</p><p>Prokaryotic genetic material is organized in a simple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circular_bacterial_chromosome" title="Circular bacterial chromosome">circular bacterial chromosome</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleoid_region" title="Nucleoid region">nucleoid region</a> of the cytoplasm. Eukaryotic genetic material is divided into different,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> linear molecules called <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a> inside a discrete nucleus, usually with additional genetic material in some organelles like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chloroplasts" title="Chloroplasts">chloroplasts</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiotic_theory" title="Endosymbiotic theory">endosymbiotic theory</a>).
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_cell" title="Human cell">human cell</a> has genetic material contained in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">nuclear genome</a>) and in the mitochondria (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_genome" title="Mitochondrial genome">mitochondrial genome</a>). In humans, the nuclear genome is divided into 46 linear DNA molecules called <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a>, including 22 <a href="/wiki/Homologous_chromosome" title="Homologous chromosome">homologous chromosome</a> pairs and a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex_chromosomes" title="Sex chromosomes">sex chromosomes</a>. The mitochondrial genome is a circular DNA molecule distinct from nuclear DNA. Although the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> is very small compared to nuclear chromosomes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> it codes for 13 proteins involved in mitochondrial energy production and specific tRNAs.
</p><p>Foreign genetic material (most commonly DNA) can also be artificially introduced into the cell by a process called <a href="/wiki/Transfection" title="Transfection">transfection</a>. This can be transient, if the DNA is not inserted into the cell's <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>, or stable, if it is. Certain <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> also insert their genetic material into the genome.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organelles">Organelles</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">Organelle</a></div>
<p>Organelles are parts of the cell that are adapted and/or specialized for carrying out one or more vital functions, analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Organ_(biology)" title="Organ (biology)">organs</a> of the human body (such as the heart, lung, and kidney, with each organ performing a different function).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> Both eukaryotic and prokaryotic cells have organelles, but prokaryotic organelles are generally simpler and are not membrane-bound.
</p><p>There are several types of organelles in a cell. Some (such as the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">Golgi apparatus</a>) are typically solitary, while others (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chloroplasts" title="Chloroplasts">chloroplasts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peroxisomes" title="Peroxisomes">peroxisomes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lysosomes" title="Lysosomes">lysosomes</a>) can be numerous (hundreds to thousands). The <a href="/wiki/Cytosol" title="Cytosol">cytosol</a> is the gelatinous fluid that fills the cell and surrounds the organelles.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eukaryotic">Eukaryotic</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HeLa_cells_stained_with_Hoechst_33258.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="459" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/HeLa_cells_stained_with_Hoechst_33258.jpg/220px-HeLa_cells_stained_with_Hoechst_33258.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/HeLa_cells_stained_with_Hoechst_33258.jpg/330px-HeLa_cells_stained_with_Hoechst_33258.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/HeLa_cells_stained_with_Hoechst_33258.jpg/440px-HeLa_cells_stained_with_Hoechst_33258.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HeLa_cells_stained_with_Hoechst_33258.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human cancer cells, specifically <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HeLa_cells" title="HeLa cells">HeLa cells</a>, with DNA stained blue. The central and rightmost cell are in <a href="/wiki/Interphase" title="Interphase">interphase</a>, so their DNA is diffuse and the entire nuclei are labelled. The cell on the left is going through <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> and its chromosomes have condensed.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><b>Cell nucleus</b>: A cell's information center, the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a> is the most conspicuous organelle found in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> cell. It houses the cell's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromosomes" title="Chromosomes">chromosomes</a>, and is the place where almost all <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> replication and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> synthesis (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a>) occur. The nucleus is spherical and separated from the cytoplasm by a double membrane called the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_envelope" title="Nuclear envelope">nuclear envelope</a>, space between these two membrane is called perinuclear space. The nuclear envelope isolates and protects a cell's DNA from various molecules that could accidentally damage its structure or interfere with its processing. During processing, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcribed</a>, or copied into a special <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a> (mRNA). This mRNA is then transported out of the nucleus, where it is translated into a specific protein molecule. The <a href="/wiki/Nucleolus" title="Nucleolus">nucleolus</a> is a specialized region within the nucleus where ribosome subunits are assembled. In prokaryotes, DNA processing takes place in the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Mitochondria and chloroplasts</b>: generate energy for the cell. <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrion" title="Mitochondrion">Mitochondria</a> are self-replicating double membrane-bound organelles that occur in various numbers, shapes, and sizes in the cytoplasm of all eukaryotic cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">Respiration</a> occurs in the cell mitochondria, which generate the cell's energy by <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">oxidative phosphorylation</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> to release energy stored in cellular nutrients (typically pertaining to <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>) to generate <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerobic_respiration" title="Aerobic respiration">aerobic respiration</a>). Mitochondria multiply by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">binary fission</a>, like prokaryotes. Chloroplasts can only be found in plants and algae, and they capture the sun's energy to make carbohydrates through <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>.</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Endomembrane_system_diagram_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="612" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Endomembrane_system_diagram_en.svg/280px-Endomembrane_system_diagram_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Endomembrane_system_diagram_en.svg/420px-Endomembrane_system_diagram_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Endomembrane_system_diagram_en.svg/560px-Endomembrane_system_diagram_en.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Endomembrane_system_diagram_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of the <a href="/wiki/Endomembrane_system" title="Endomembrane system">endomembrane system</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><b>Endoplasmic reticulum</b>: The <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">endoplasmic reticulum</a> (ER) is a transport network for molecules targeted for certain modifications and specific destinations, as compared to molecules that float freely in the cytoplasm. The ER has two forms: the rough ER, which has ribosomes on its surface that secrete proteins into the ER, and the smooth ER, which lacks ribosomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> The smooth ER plays a role in calcium sequestration and release and also helps in synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a>.</li>
<li><b>Golgi apparatus</b>: The primary function of the Golgi apparatus is to process and package the <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> that are synthesized by the cell.</li>
<li><b>Lysosomes and peroxisomes</b>: <a href="/wiki/Lysosome" title="Lysosome">Lysosomes</a> contain <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzyme" title="Digestive enzyme">digestive enzymes</a> (acid <a href="/wiki/Hydrolase" title="Hydrolase">hydrolases</a>). They digest excess or worn-out <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a>, food particles, and engulfed <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>. <a href="/wiki/Peroxisome" title="Peroxisome">Peroxisomes</a> have enzymes that rid the cell of toxic <a href="/wiki/Peroxide" title="Peroxide">peroxides</a>, Lysosomes are optimally active at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Acidic_pH&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Acidic pH (page does not exist)">acidic pH</a>. The cell could not house these destructive enzymes if they were not contained in a membrane-bound system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Centrosome</b>: the cytoskeleton organizer: The <a href="/wiki/Centrosome" title="Centrosome">centrosome</a> produces the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microtubules" title="Microtubules">microtubules</a> of a cell – a key component of the <a href="/wiki/Cytoskeleton" title="Cytoskeleton">cytoskeleton</a>. It directs the transport through the <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">ER</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">Golgi apparatus</a>. Centrosomes are composed of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrioles" title="Centrioles">centrioles</a> which lie perpendicular to each other in which each has an organization like a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cart_wheel" title="Cart wheel">cartwheel</a>, which separate during <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a> and help in the formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitotic_spindle" title="Mitotic spindle">mitotic spindle</a>. A single centrosome is present in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Animal_cell" title="Animal cell">animal cells</a>. They are also found in some fungi and algae cells.</li>
<li><b>Vacuoles</b>: <a href="/wiki/Vacuole" title="Vacuole">Vacuoles</a> sequester waste products and in plant cells store water. They are often described as liquid filled spaces and are surrounded by a membrane. Some cells, most notably <i><a href="/wiki/Amoeba_(genus)" title="Amoeba (genus)">Amoeba</a></i>, have contractile vacuoles, which can pump water out of the cell if there is too much water. The vacuoles of plant cells and fungal cells are usually larger than those of animal cells. Vacuoles of plant cells are surrounded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonoplast" title="Tonoplast">tonoplast</a> which helps in transport of ions and other substances against concentration gradients.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eukaryotic_and_prokaryotic">Eukaryotic and prokaryotic</span></h4>
<ul><li><b>Ribosomes</b>: The <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosome</a> is a large complex of <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> They each consist of two subunits, and act as an assembly line where RNA from the nucleus is used to synthesise proteins from amino acids. Ribosomes can be found either floating freely or bound to a membrane (the rough endoplasmatic reticulum in eukaryotes, or the cell membrane in prokaryotes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><b>Plastids</b>: <a href="/wiki/Plastid" title="Plastid">Plastid</a> are  membrane-bound organelle generally found in plant cells and <a href="/wiki/Euglenid" title="Euglenid">euglenoids</a> and contain specific <i>pigments</i>, thus affecting the colour of the plant and organism. And these pigments also helps in food storage and tapping of light energy. There are three types of plastids based upon the specific pigments. <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">Chloroplasts</a>(contains <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> and some carotenoid pigments which helps in the tapping of light energy during photosynthesis), <a href="/wiki/Chromoplast" title="Chromoplast">Chromoplasts</a>(contains fat-soluble <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoid</a> pigments like orange carotene and yellow xanthophylls which helps in synthesis and storage), <a href="/wiki/Leucoplast" title="Leucoplast">Leucoplasts</a>(are non-pigmented plastids and helps in storage of nutrients).</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structures_outside_the_cell_membrane">Structures outside the cell membrane</span></h2>
<p>Many cells also have structures which exist wholly or partially outside the cell membrane. These structures are notable because they are not protected from the external environment by the <a href="/wiki/Semipermeable_membrane" title="Semipermeable membrane">semipermeable cell membrane</a>. In order to assemble these structures, their components must be carried across the cell membrane by export processes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell_wall">Cell wall</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">Cell wall</a></div>
<p>Many types of prokaryotic and eukaryotic cells have a <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a>. The cell wall acts to protect the cell mechanically and chemically from its environment, and is an additional layer of protection to the cell membrane. Different types of cell have cell walls made up of different materials; plant cell walls are primarily made up of <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, fungi cell walls are made up of <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitin</a> and bacteria cell walls are made up of <a href="/wiki/Peptidoglycan" title="Peptidoglycan">peptidoglycan</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prokaryotic">Prokaryotic</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Capsule">Capsule</span></h4>
<p>A gelatinous <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_capsule" title="Bacterial capsule">capsule</a> is present in some bacteria outside the cell membrane and cell wall. The capsule may be <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharide</a> as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pneumococci" title="Pneumococci">pneumococci</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meningococci" title="Meningococci">meningococci</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polypeptide" title="Polypeptide">polypeptide</a> as <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">Bacillus anthracis</a></i> or <a href="/wiki/Hyaluronic_acid" title="Hyaluronic acid">hyaluronic acid</a> as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Streptococci" title="Streptococci">streptococci</a>.
Capsules are not marked by normal staining protocols and can be detected by <a href="/wiki/India_ink#Uses_other_than_writing" title="India ink">India ink</a> or <a href="/wiki/Methyl_blue" title="Methyl blue">methyl blue</a>; which allows for higher contrast between the cells for observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 87">: 87 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flagella">Flagella</span></h4>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagella" title="Flagella">Flagella</a> are organelles for cellular mobility. The bacterial flagellum stretches from cytoplasm through the cell membrane(s) and extrudes through the cell wall. They are long and thick thread-like appendages, protein in nature. A different type of flagellum is found in archaea and a different type is found in eukaryotes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fimbriae">Fimbriae</span></h4>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fimbria_(bacteriology)" title="Fimbria (bacteriology)">fimbria</a> (plural fimbriae also known as a <a href="/wiki/Pilus" title="Pilus">pilus</a>, plural pili) is a short, thin, hair-like filament found on the surface of bacteria. Fimbriae are formed of a protein called <a href="/wiki/Pilin" title="Pilin">pilin</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigenic" title="Antigenic">antigenic</a>) and are responsible for the attachment of bacteria to specific receptors on human cells (<a href="/wiki/Cell_adhesion" title="Cell adhesion">cell adhesion</a>). There are special types of pili involved in <a href="/wiki/Bacterial_conjugation" title="Bacterial conjugation">bacterial conjugation</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cellular_processes">Cellular processes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_cell_growth_types.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="694" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Three_cell_growth_types.svg/280px-Three_cell_growth_types.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Three_cell_growth_types.svg/420px-Three_cell_growth_types.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Three_cell_growth_types.svg/560px-Three_cell_growth_types.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_cell_growth_types.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotes" title="Prokaryotes">Prokaryotes</a> divide by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">binary fission</a>, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotes" title="Eukaryotes">eukaryotes</a> divide by <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Replication">Replication</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">Cell division</a></div>
<p>Cell division involves a single cell (called a <i>mother cell</i>) dividing into two daughter cells. This leads to growth in <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">multicellular organisms</a> (the growth of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_tissue" title="Biological tissue">tissue</a>) and to procreation (<a href="/wiki/Vegetative_reproduction" title="Vegetative reproduction">vegetative reproduction</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Unicellular_organism" title="Unicellular organism">unicellular organisms</a>. <a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">Prokaryotic</a> cells divide by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_fission" title="Binary fission">binary fission</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic</a> cells usually undergo a process of nuclear division, called <a href="/wiki/Mitosis" title="Mitosis">mitosis</a>, followed by division of the cell, called <a href="/wiki/Cytokinesis" title="Cytokinesis">cytokinesis</a>. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a> cell may also undergo <a href="/wiki/Meiosis" title="Meiosis">meiosis</a> to produce haploid cells, usually four. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">Haploid</a> cells serve as <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a> in multicellular organisms, fusing to form new diploid cells.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/DNA_replication" title="DNA replication">DNA replication</a>, or the process of duplicating a cell's genome,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> always happens when a cell divides through mitosis or binary fission. This occurs during the S phase of the <a href="/wiki/Cell_cycle" title="Cell cycle">cell cycle</a>.
</p><p>In meiosis, the DNA is replicated only once, while the cell divides twice. DNA replication only occurs before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiosis_I" title="Meiosis I">meiosis I</a>. DNA replication does not occur when the cells divide the second time, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiosis_II" title="Meiosis II">meiosis II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Replication, like all cellular activities, requires specialized proteins for carrying out the job.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-16"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Catabolism_schematic.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/220px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/330px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Catabolism_schematic.svg/440px-Catabolism_schematic.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Catabolism_schematic.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An outline of the <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="DNA_repair">DNA repair</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a></div>
<p>In general, cells of all organisms contain enzyme systems that scan their DNA for <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">DNA damage</a> and carry out <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">repair processes</a> when damage is detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Diverse repair processes have evolved in organisms ranging from bacteria to humans. The widespread prevalence of these repair processes indicates the importance of maintaining cellular DNA in an undamaged state in order to avoid cell death or errors of replication due to damage that could lead to <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli"><i>E. coli</i></a> bacteria are a well-studied example of a cellular organism with diverse well-defined <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA repair</a> processes. These include: (1) <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide_excision_repair" title="Nucleotide excision repair">nucleotide excision repair</a>, (2) <a href="/wiki/DNA_mismatch_repair" title="DNA mismatch repair">DNA mismatch repair</a>, (3) <a href="/wiki/Non-homologous_end_joining" title="Non-homologous end joining">non-homologous end joining</a> of double-strand breaks, (4) <a href="/wiki/Homologous_recombination" title="Homologous recombination">recombinational repair</a> and (5) light-dependent repair (<a href="/wiki/Photolyase" title="Photolyase">photoreactivation</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_and_metabolism">Growth and metabolism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proteinsynthesis.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="361" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Proteinsynthesis.png/220px-Proteinsynthesis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/09/Proteinsynthesis.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proteinsynthesis.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An overview of protein synthesis.<br/>Within the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a> of the cell (<i>light blue</i>), <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> (DNA, <i>dark blue</i>) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcribed</a> into <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>. This RNA is then subject to post-transcriptional modification and control, resulting in a mature <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MRNA" title="MRNA">mRNA</a> (<i>red</i>) that is then transported out of the nucleus and into the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> (<i>peach</i>), where it undergoes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translation_(genetics)" title="Translation (genetics)">translation</a> into a protein. mRNA is translated by <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a> (<i>purple</i>) that match the three-base <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Codon" title="Codon">codons</a> of the mRNA to the three-base anti-codons of the appropriate <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">tRNA</a>. Newly synthesized proteins (<i>black</i>) are often further modified, such as by binding to an effector molecule (<i>orange</i>), to become fully active.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">Cell growth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">Metabolism</a></div>
<p>Between successive cell divisions, cells grow through the functioning of cellular metabolism. Cell metabolism is the process by which individual cells process nutrient molecules. Metabolism has two distinct divisions: <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a>, in which the cell breaks down complex molecules to produce energy and <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing power</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a>, in which the cell uses energy and reducing power to construct complex molecules and perform other biological functions.
Complex sugars consumed by the organism can be broken down into simpler sugar molecules called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monosaccharides" title="Monosaccharides">monosaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>. Once inside the cell, glucose is broken down to make adenosine triphosphate (<a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBI_4-17"><a href="#cite_note-NCBI-4">[4]</a></sup> a molecule that possesses readily available energy, through two different pathways.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Protein_synthesis">Protein synthesis</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Protein_biosynthesis" title="Protein biosynthesis">Protein biosynthesis</a></div>
<p>Cells are capable of synthesizing new proteins, which are essential for the modulation and maintenance of cellular activities. This process involves the formation of new protein molecules from <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> building blocks based on information encoded in DNA/RNA. Protein synthesis generally consists of two major steps: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Translation_(genetics)" title="Translation (genetics)">translation</a>.
</p><p>Transcription is the process where genetic information in DNA is used to produce a complementary RNA strand. This RNA strand is then processed to give <a href="/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a> (mRNA), which is free to migrate through the cell. mRNA molecules bind to protein-RNA complexes called <a href="/wiki/Ribosome" title="Ribosome">ribosomes</a> located in the <a href="/wiki/Cytosol" title="Cytosol">cytosol</a>, where they are translated into polypeptide sequences. The ribosome mediates the formation of a polypeptide sequence based on the mRNA sequence. The mRNA sequence directly relates to the polypeptide sequence by binding to <a href="/wiki/Transfer_RNA" title="Transfer RNA">transfer RNA</a> (tRNA) adapter molecules in binding pockets within the ribosome. The new polypeptide then folds into a functional three-dimensional protein molecule.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motility">Motility</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Motility" title="Motility">Motility</a></div>
<p>Unicellular organisms can move in order to find food or escape predators. Common mechanisms of motion include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagella" title="Flagella">flagella</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cilia" title="Cilia">cilia</a>.
</p><p>In multicellular organisms, cells can move during processes such as wound healing, the immune response and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cancer_metastasis" title="Cancer metastasis">cancer metastasis</a>. For example, in wound healing in animals, white blood cells move to the wound site to kill the microorganisms that cause infection. Cell motility involves many receptors, crosslinking, bundling, binding, adhesion, motor and other proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The process is divided into three steps – protrusion of the leading edge of the cell, adhesion of the leading edge and de-adhesion at the cell body and rear, and cytoskeletal contraction to pull the cell forward. Each step is driven by physical forces generated by unique segments of the cytoskeleton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alberts2_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alberts2-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ananthakrishnan_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ananthakrishnan-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Navigation.2C_control_and_communication"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation,_control_and_communication">Navigation, control and communication</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cybernetics#In_biology" title="Cybernetics">Cybernetics § In biology</a></div>
<p>In August 2020, scientists described one way cells – in particular cells of a slime mold and mouse pancreatic cancer–derived cells – are able to <a href="/wiki/Chemotaxis" title="Chemotaxis">navigate</a> efficiently through a body and identify the best routes through complex mazes: generating gradients after breaking down diffused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemoattractant" title="Chemoattractant">chemoattractants</a> which enable them to sense upcoming maze junctions before reaching them, including around corners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Multicellularity">Multicellularity</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">Multicellular organism</a></div>
<h3><span id="Cell_specialization.2Fdifferentiation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cell_specialization/differentiation">Cell specialization/differentiation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">Cellular differentiation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C_elegans_stained.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="897" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/C_elegans_stained.jpg/170px-C_elegans_stained.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/C_elegans_stained.jpg/255px-C_elegans_stained.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/C_elegans_stained.jpg/340px-C_elegans_stained.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C_elegans_stained.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Staining of a <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">Caenorhabditis elegans</a></i> highlights the nuclei of its cells.</div></div></div>
<p>Multicellular organisms are <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> that consist of more than one cell, in contrast to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-celled_organism" title="Single-celled organism">single-celled organisms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In complex multicellular organisms, cells specialize into different <a href="/wiki/Cell_type" title="Cell type">cell types</a> that are adapted to particular functions. In mammals, major cell types include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_cells" title="Skin cells">skin cells</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle_cells" title="Muscle cells">muscle cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood_cell" title="Blood cell">blood cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fibroblast" title="Fibroblast">fibroblasts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_cells" title="Stem cells">stem cells</a>, and others. Cell types differ both in appearance and function, yet are <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetically</a> identical. Cells are able to be of the same <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a> but of different cell type due to the differential <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_gene_expression" title="Regulation of gene expression">expression</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> they contain.
</p><p>Most distinct cell types arise from a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totipotent" title="Totipotent">totipotent</a> cell, called a <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygote</a>, that <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">differentiates</a> into hundreds of different cell types during the course of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Development_(biology)" title="Development (biology)">development</a>. Differentiation of cells is driven by different environmental cues (such as cell–cell interaction) and intrinsic differences (such as those caused by the uneven distribution of <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> during <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">division</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_multicellularity">Origin of multicellularity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Multicellular_organism" title="Multicellular organism">Multicellular organism</a></div>
<p>Multicellularity has evolved independently at least 25 times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosberg2007_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grosberg2007-36">[36]</a></sup> including in some prokaryotes, like <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myxobacteria" title="Myxobacteria">myxobacteria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actinomycetes" title="Actinomycetes">actinomycetes</a>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Magnetoglobus_multicellularis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Magnetoglobus multicellularis (page does not exist)">Magnetoglobus multicellularis</a></i>, or <i><a href="/wiki/Methanosarcina" title="Methanosarcina">Methanosarcina</a></i>. However, complex multicellular organisms evolved only in six eukaryotic groups: animals, fungi, brown algae, red algae, green algae, and plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21351878_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21351878-37">[37]</a></sup> It evolved repeatedly for plants (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chloroplastida" title="Chloroplastida">Chloroplastida</a>), once or twice for <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, once for <a href="/wiki/Brown_algae" title="Brown algae">brown algae</a>, and perhaps several times for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mycetozoa" title="Mycetozoa">slime molds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Red_algae" title="Red algae">red algae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Multicellularity may have evolved from <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a> of interdependent organisms, from <a href="/wiki/Cellularization" title="Cellularization">cellularization</a>, or from organisms in <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic relationships</a>.
</p><p>The first evidence of multicellularity is from <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>-like organisms that lived between 3 and 3.5 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosberg2007_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grosberg2007-36">[36]</a></sup> Other early fossils of multicellular organisms include the contested <a href="/wiki/Grypania" title="Grypania">Grypania</a> spiralis and the fossils of the black shales of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeoproterozoic" title="Palaeoproterozoic">Palaeoproterozoic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francevillian_Group_Fossil" title="Francevillian Group Fossil">Francevillian Group Fossil</a> B Formation in <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The evolution of multicellularity from unicellular ancestors has been replicated in the laboratory, in <a href="/wiki/Experimental_evolution" title="Experimental evolution">evolution experiments</a> using predation as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selective_pressure" title="Selective pressure">selective pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosberg2007_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grosberg2007-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">Evolutionary history of life</a></div>
<p>The origin of cells has to do with the <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">origin of life</a>, which began the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_evolution" title="Timeline of evolution">history of life</a> on Earth.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_first_cell">Origin of the first cell</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stromatolites.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Stromatolites.jpg/220px-Stromatolites.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Stromatolites.jpg/330px-Stromatolites.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Stromatolites.jpg/440px-Stromatolites.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stromatolites.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stromatolites" title="Stromatolites">Stromatolites</a> are left behind by <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, also called blue-green algae. They are the oldest known fossils of life on Earth. This one-billion-year-old fossil is from <a href="/wiki/Glacier_National_Park_(U.S.)" title="Glacier National Park (U.S.)">Glacier National Park</a> in the United States.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">Abiogenesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_cells" title="Evolution of cells">Evolution of cells</a></div>
<p>There are several theories about the origin of small molecules that led to life on the <a href="/wiki/Early_Earth" title="Early Earth">early Earth</a>. They may have been carried to Earth on meteorites (see <a href="/wiki/Murchison_meteorite" title="Murchison meteorite">Murchison meteorite</a>), created at <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">deep-sea vents</a>, or synthesized by lightning in a reducing atmosphere (see <a href="/wiki/Miller%E2%80%93Urey_experiment" title="Miller–Urey experiment">Miller–Urey experiment</a>). There is little experimental data defining what the first self-replicating forms were. <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> is thought to be the earliest self-replicating molecule, as it is capable of both storing genetic information and catalyzing chemical reactions (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RNA_world_hypothesis" title="RNA world hypothesis">RNA world hypothesis</a>), but some other entity with the potential to self-replicate could have preceded RNA, such as <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis#Clay_hypothesis" title="Abiogenesis">clay</a> or <a href="/wiki/Peptide_nucleic_acid" title="Peptide nucleic acid">peptide nucleic acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrgelLE_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-OrgelLE-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cells emerged at least 3.5 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin1_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin1-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Origin2_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RavenJohnson2002_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-RavenJohnson2002-43">[43]</a></sup> The current belief is that these cells were <a href="/wiki/Heterotroph" title="Heterotroph">heterotrophs</a>. The early cell membranes were probably more simple and permeable than modern ones, with only a single fatty acid chain per lipid. Lipids are known to spontaneously form bilayered <a href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology_and_chemistry)" title="Vesicle (biology and chemistry)">vesicles</a> in water, and could have preceded RNA, but the first cell membranes could also have been produced by catalytic RNA, or even have required structural proteins before they could form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_eukaryotic_cells">Origin of eukaryotic cells</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_sexual_reproduction" title="Evolution of sexual reproduction">Evolution of sexual reproduction</a></div>
<p>The eukaryotic cell seems to have evolved from a <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic community</a> of prokaryotic cells. DNA-bearing organelles like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chloroplasts" title="Chloroplasts">chloroplasts</a> are descended from ancient symbiotic oxygen-breathing <a href="/wiki/Alphaproteobacteria" title="Alphaproteobacteria">Alphaproteobacteria</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">Cyanobacteria</a>", respectively, which were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiotic_theory" title="Endosymbiotic theory">endosymbiosed</a> by an ancestral <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaean</a> prokaryote.
</p><p>There is still considerable debate about whether organelles like the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenosome" title="Hydrogenosome">hydrogenosome</a> predated the origin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>, or vice versa: see the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_hypothesis" title="Hydrogen hypothesis">hydrogen hypothesis</a> for the origin of eukaryotic cells.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_research">History of research</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cell_theory" title="Cell theory">Cell theory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RobertHookeMicrographia1665.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/RobertHookeMicrographia1665.jpg/220px-RobertHookeMicrographia1665.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/RobertHookeMicrographia1665.jpg/330px-RobertHookeMicrographia1665.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/RobertHookeMicrographia1665.jpg/440px-RobertHookeMicrographia1665.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RobertHookeMicrographia1665.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert Hooke's drawing of cells in <a href="/wiki/Cork_(material)" title="Cork (material)">cork</a>, 1665</div></div></div>
<ul><li>1632–1723: <a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a> taught himself to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lenses</a>, constructed basic <a href="/wiki/Optical_microscope" title="Optical microscope">optical microscopes</a> and drew protozoa, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Vorticella" title="Vorticella">Vorticella</a></i> from rain water, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterium" title="Bacterium">bacteria</a> from his own mouth.</li>
<li>1665: <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> discovered cells in <a href="/wiki/Cork_(material)" title="Cork (material)">cork</a>, then in living plant tissue using an early compound microscope. He coined the term <i>cell</i> (from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>cellula</i>, meaning "small room"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr12_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-npr12-1">[1]</a></sup>) in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Micrographia" title="Micrographia">Micrographia</a></i> (1665).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hooke_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hooke-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>1839: <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Schwann" title="Theodor Schwann">Theodor Schwann</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Jakob_Schleiden" title="Matthias Jakob Schleiden">Matthias Jakob Schleiden</a> elucidated the principle that plants and animals are made of cells, concluding that cells are a common unit of structure and development, and thus founding the cell theory.</li>
<li>1855: <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Virchow" title="Rudolf Virchow">Rudolf Virchow</a> stated that new cells come from pre-existing cells by <a href="/wiki/Cell_division" title="Cell division">cell division</a> (<i>omnis cellula ex cellula</i>).</li>
<li>1859: The belief that life forms can occur spontaneously (<i><a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">generatio spontanea</a></i>) was contradicted by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> (1822–1895) (although <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Redi" title="Francesco Redi">Francesco Redi</a> had performed an experiment in 1668 that suggested the same conclusion).</li>
<li>1931: <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Ruska" title="Ernst Ruska">Ernst Ruska</a> built the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscope" title="Transmission electron microscope">transmission electron microscope</a> (TEM) at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Berlin" title="University of Berlin">University of Berlin</a>. By 1935, he had built an EM with twice the resolution of a light microscope, revealing previously unresolvable organelles.</li>
<li>1953: Based on <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a>'s work, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_D._Watson" title="James D. Watson">Watson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Crick</a> made their first announcement on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_helix" title="Double helix">double helix</a> structure of DNA.</li>
<li>1981: <a href="/wiki/Lynn_Margulis" title="Lynn Margulis">Lynn Margulis</a> published <i>Symbiosis in Cell Evolution</i> detailing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endosymbiotic_theory" title="Endosymbiotic theory">endosymbiotic theory</a>.</li></ul>

The Alberts text discusses how the "cellular building blocks" move to shape developing <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryos</a>. It is also common to describe small molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> as "<a class="external text" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Search&amp;db=books&amp;doptcmdl=GenBookHL&amp;term=%22all+cells%22+AND+mboc4%5Bbook%5D+AND+372023%5Buid%5D&amp;rid=mboc4.section.4#23" rel="nofollow">molecular building blocks</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200122055404/https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books?cmd=Search&amp;doptcmdl=GenBookHL&amp;term=%22all%2Bcells%22%2BAND%2Bmboc4%5Bbook%5D%2BAND%2B372023%5Buid%5D&amp;rid=mboc4.section.4#23" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2020-01-22 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>".</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFTero1990">Tero AC (1990). <i>Achiever's Biology</i>. I could exceedingly plainly perceive it to be all perforated and porous, much like a Honey-comb, but that the pores of it were not regular [...] these pores, or cells, [...] were indeed the first microscopical pores I ever saw, and perhaps, that were ever seen, for I had not met with any Writer or Person, that had made any mention of them before this ... " – Hooke describing his observations on a thin slice of cork. See also:
 <a class="external text" href="http://www.ucmp.berkeley.edu/history/hooke.html" rel="nofollow">Robert Hooke</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/19970606013455/http://www.ucmp.berkeley.edu/history/hooke.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 1997-06-06 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></span>
</li>
